<div id="review_2520724762" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2520724762" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2520724762?book_show_action=true">Sep 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=pre-80s-sf">pre-80s-sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2520724762" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2270513114071870054"><i>“The blast and sound wave covered them, submerging all other sound and feeling. Again the kitchenware and glasses and china danced. A delicate vase of Viennese crystal crumpled into powder and shards on the mantle. The glass protecting a meticulous and vivid still life, a water color by Lee Adams, shattered in its frame with a loud report.”</i><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1180411832l/1037236.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>This book is a classic of the “post-apocalypse” genre, a granddaddy I suppose. First published in 1959, still in print today, with loads of online study guide</span>
  <span id="freeText2270513114071870054" style="display:none"><i>“The blast and sound wave covered them, submerging all other sound and feeling. Again the kitchenware and glasses and china danced. A delicate vase of Viennese crystal crumpled into powder and shards on the mantle. The glass protecting a meticulous and vivid still life, a water color by Lee Adams, shattered in its frame with a loud report.”</i><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1180411832l/1037236.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>This book is a classic of the “post-apocalypse” genre, a granddaddy I suppose. First published in 1959, still in print today, with loads of online study guides for perusal. Not too shabby! The most exciting part of <i> Alas, Babylon</i> is the nuclear attack. Don’t worry, this is not a spoiler, it is the whole basis of the book. The attack happens early in the narrative and the rest of the book concerns the post-apocalypse life after this attack.<br><br>The novel starts off portraying the mundane lives of the residents of Fort Repose, a small town in Florida. If I had not read the synopsis beforehand I would not have known where the book was going and why it is labeled as sci-fi. This becomes clearer as the main characters struggle to adapt to a post-apocalyptic life. Fortunately for them, Fort Repose was not directly nuked and escapes the worst of the radiation; they even have electricity and plumbing for a few days.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536214496i/26259173._SY540_.jpg" width="300" height="508" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>I like the believable development of the post-civilization hardship. Money becomes worthless, hunting and fishing become necessary, homemade soap, beverages etc. begin to be concocted, even “stand and deliver” highwaymen become a thing again. Some people even find more meaning and fulfillment in their hitherto uneventful lives after the attack. While this book is categorized as science fiction, there is nothing particularly “sci-fi” about it. No futuristic tech, spaceships, extraterrestrials etc. Science is only used for expositions, how radioactivity can seep into objects, the effect of radioactive poisoning, how an irradiated ring affects the skin etc. <br><br>The details of post-apocalyptic life here are very well thought out by the author. Life would be hell, but good people can persevere, pick up the pieces and slowly rebuild. It is a very interesting read but if you are looking for a “thriller” this ain’t it. It is more of a realistic exploration of life under such dire circumstances. Some of the main characters are fairly well developed but they do not really come alive off the page for me. Authors like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16648124.Octavia_Butler" title="Octavia Butler" rel="nofollow noopener"> Octavia Butler</a> can breathe life into their characters in just a few paragraphs but I don’t think this is Pat Frank’s forte; this makes the narrative a little less compelling for me. Nevertheless, we should all be grateful to Mr. Frank for penning this cautionary tale and hope that enough people take heed of the caution.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536214496i/26259174.jpg" alt="m cloud" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><b>Notes:</b><br>• The pastoral post-apocalyptic landscape reminds me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/91109.The_Long_Tomorrow" title="The Long Tomorrow by Leigh Brackett" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Long Tomorrow</a>.<br>• John Lennon was much disturbed by this book and it led to his anti-war fervor (<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alas,_Babylon" rel="nofollow noopener">thank you Wikipedia!</a>). He must have imagined all sorts of things…<br><br><b>Quotes:</b><br><i>“Still, there was no sound quite like a siren wailing its air-raid alarm to spur people to constructive action-or paralyze them in fear.”<br><br>“With the use of the hydrogen bomb, the Christian era was dead, and with it must die the tradition of the Good Samaritan.”<br><br>“His theory of challenge and response applies not only to nations, but to individuals. Some nations and some people melt in the heat of crisis and come apart like fat in the pan. Others meet the challenge and harden.”<br><br>“He swam in a sea of money, and when money was transmuted back into paper he was left gasping and confused, and he died.”<br><br>“Dozens of people killed themselves for the same reason. They created and lived in an environment of paper profits, and when paper returned to paper they had to kill themselves, not realizing that their environment was unnatural and artificial.”<br><br>“Treasury in Washington, Wall Street, and Federal Reserve banks everywhere, all were now radioactive ash.”<br><br>“It was strange, she thought, pedaling steadily, that it should require a holocaust to make her own life worth living.”</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="2270513114071870054" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_586789155" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/586789155" />

              <span id="reviewTextContainer586789155" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer17648333765984134373">Praise the Lord and Pass the Ammunition! <br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=LJfJPxLntZU'>https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=LJfJP...</a><br/><br/>Honestly, I'm of two minds. For a nuclear holocaust dystopia from 1959, it's probably top notch. A lot of fans attest to it. It's also full of good characters and solid 1959 survivability thought. No complaints about the science, either. In fact, the whole situation and character feel is quite a bit like the Walking Dead. Solid.<br/><br/>So what's my complaint? <br/><br/>This outcome could only have happened in 1959 and it was egregiou</span>
  <span id="freeText17648333765984134373" style="display:none">Praise the Lord and Pass the Ammunition! <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=LJfJPxLntZU">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=LJfJP...</a><br /><br />Honestly, I'm of two minds. For a nuclear holocaust dystopia from 1959, it's probably top notch. A lot of fans attest to it. It's also full of good characters and solid 1959 survivability thought. No complaints about the science, either. In fact, the whole situation and character feel is quite a bit like the Walking Dead. Solid.<br /><br />So what's my complaint? <br /><br />This outcome could only have happened in 1959 and it was egregiously optimistic to boot. The nuclear stockpiles and deployment might not have made it to total annihilation levels by then so lobbing all the arsenal might not have been an all or nothing thing at this point in history. Plus there was still a lot of the previous generation (read pre-electricity) tools available to peeps at this time, even if they were getting rather old.<br /><br />That, and it was mild as hell compared to most dystopian books I've read or even movies. Again. 1959. And the totally unbelievable optimistic ending? Yeah. 1959.<br /><br />But that's just it. It's hard to activate my magical belief hoodie after having lived through so many dystopians and expecting something even as grand as Fallout. <br /><br />At the very LEAST, however, it shows me how much society has changed since then. 60 years. Now, all we do is expect unending rapes and mass murder when the world ends. This book tells the opposite and optimistic story. Despite the mass death. *shakes head*<br /><br />Props for its time, though! Want some Nuka Cola?</span>

<div id="review_248462658" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/248462658" />
    <a title="Marvin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2160536-marvin"><img alt="Marvin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485672728p2/2160536.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/248462658?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marvin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marvin" href="/user/show/2160536-marvin">Marvin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780553115024" href="/book/show/38170.Alas_Babylon">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer248462658" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15801645712099231518"><i>Alas, Babylon</i> was written in 1959 and is part of what I call the Trifecta of Nuclear Cold War novels. The first is <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/354259.Fail_Safe' title='Fail Safe by Eugene Burdick' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fail Safe</a> which addresses how Nuclear War can be easily triggered though human error and simple stupidity. The second is <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38180.On_the_Beach' title='On the Beach by Nevil Shute' rel='nofollow noopener'>On the Beach</a> which deals with the possible end of humankind due to nuclear war. The third is <i>Alas Babylon</i> which takes play immediately after a first nuclear attack and deals with the hardships of surviving a nuclear attack. All three together adequately portrays t</span>
  <span id="freeText15801645712099231518" style="display:none"><i>Alas, Babylon</i> was written in 1959 and is part of what I call the Trifecta of Nuclear Cold War novels. The first is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/354259.Fail_Safe" title="Fail Safe by Eugene Burdick" rel="nofollow noopener">Fail Safe</a> which addresses how Nuclear War can be easily triggered though human error and simple stupidity. The second is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38180.On_the_Beach" title="On the Beach by Nevil Shute" rel="nofollow noopener">On the Beach</a> which deals with the possible end of humankind due to nuclear war. The third is <i>Alas Babylon</i> which takes play immediately after a first nuclear attack and deals with the hardships of surviving a nuclear attack. All three together adequately portrays the fear and apprehension of people in the 50s. <i>Alas Babylon</i> is the most optimistic of the three in the sense that we in the 50s did not yet understand how devastating Nuclear War could actually be. Nuclear Winter and other results of a nuclear holocaust were not yet know, or at least not publicly known. Yet the devastation of all-out nuclear war was still quite alarming. <i>Alas, Babylon</i>, in its way, is a classic novel, one of the best of its era. It focuses on a small Florida town immediately following the start of a war between the US and the Soviet Union. The author beautifully sets up the sociopolitical environment but wisely keeps the focus on the actiona of the townspeople. Faced with radiation fallout, a loss of law enforcement, isolation, diseases and famine, among other things, they do as well as they can, never knowing if they are facing the end of civilization. Some reader may think the author is too optimistic and they may be right. Yet I think the author caught the time and mood realistically. My one complaint would be the inherent racism throughout the novel yet this may be on of those books that you may need to keep the period in mind and overlook some disturbing comments and assumptions. Nonetheless, <i>Alas Babylon</i> can be read as an historical document of the the cold war mentality and as a testament to the hope of survival in war time, even when hope is elusive.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15801645712099231518" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_18227648" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18227648" />
    <a title="Werner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/903390-werner"><img alt="Werner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1216337931p2/903390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18227648?book_show_action=true">Mar 20, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Werner" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Werner" href="/user/show/903390-werner">Werner</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of post-apocalyptic scenarios</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/903390-werner?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18227648" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2448026441926744267">Even as a kid and a teen, I've always had a keen interest in fictional and dramatic post-apocalyptic scenarios; I've never tried to analyze why, but perhaps the appeal of this kind of speculation is that it speaks to deep-seated modern fears and concerns about the future that all of us feel. It's a theme that's been around in science fiction since the early 19th century. In the aftermath of Hiroshima and Nagasaki, and under the tension of the Cold War, fear of nuclear war was pervasive, not only</span>
  <span id="freeText2448026441926744267" style="display:none">Even as a kid and a teen, I've always had a keen interest in fictional and dramatic post-apocalyptic scenarios; I've never tried to analyze why, but perhaps the appeal of this kind of speculation is that it speaks to deep-seated modern fears and concerns about the future that all of us feel. It's a theme that's been around in science fiction since the early 19th century. In the aftermath of Hiroshima and Nagasaki, and under the tension of the Cold War, fear of nuclear war was pervasive, not only in the SF community but among the general public, and nuclear holocaust became the typical agent of apocalypse in this sub-genre during that era. This is one of the earliest and best novels with this premise.<br /><br />There's some set-up of our small-town setting (Fort Repose, Florida) and introduction of important characters at the beginning of this novel, and some treatment of the nuclear war itself. But it's over in less than a day; the concern of the author is how people cope in the aftermath. Not a military target, Fort Repose wasn't bombed. But overnight, the electrical power grid is gone, the economic infrastructure that brought in food and fuel, etc. from outside the community is gone, and paper money is worthless. Deadly radiation is a potential problem. Food (not to mention insulin for diabetics) can't be refrigerated anymore. If you want to get anywhere, you walk or pedal a bike. Most of these conditions would exist in any small American town in the event of a technological collapse today; so for a 1959 book, it's surprisingly relevant to present possibilities. (The main difference, of course, is that in Fort Repose there are no computers and no Internet to disrupt, so that isn't an issue.)<br /><br />This is sociological science fiction at its best, a revealing look at how individuals and a community might cope with unprecedented challenge and hardship. (Or fail to cope --some people commit suicide or descend into madness.) For some people, it's an opportunity to turn into murderous brigands. Not everybody who tries to survive sanely and decently will live to do so. But others rise to the challenge of day-to-day survival and find strength to go on. And for our protagonist, hard-drinking and rather feckless young lawyer Randy Bragg, the crisis is the catalyst that brings the latent leadership abilities and sense of responsibility he didn't know he had to the fore. For all the survivors of Fort Repose, the lesson of their harsh new world is that to overcome, the things you need most are cooperation, community, family loyalty, guts, capacity to take adversity without whining and self-pity, and willingness to work hard. Those are the virtues we most need in any setting. One of my Goodreads friends called the book "depressing;" other reviewers have complained that it's too "cheery," in that it's not pessimistic enough (since Frank refrains from annihilating the whole human race, or doesn't kill off enough characters). But I wouldn't use either term. It treats the horror of the situation too realistically to be Pollyanna-like. But the author's trust in the capacity of human nature to meet the challenges facing it keeps the reader from despair.<br /><br />Set as it is in the Jim Crow South (at what, in the real world, was the cusp of the Civil Rights movement), race issues play a role in the novel. Frank, and through him Randy, both exhibit relatively enlightened attitudes for white Southerners of that era (at least, I'm surmising that Frank was a Southerner, since he evokes his setting with the ease of someone who was apparently at home there). It's interesting, and one of the better messages of the novel, to see some of the segregationist attitudes of the book characters dissolve in the face of the common peril and need. Though this isn't a Christian novel, religion is treated respectfully here (one character is a black preacher) and the role of faith in the community is portrayed positively. There's not much bad language, no explicit sex (and no glorification of loose sex; the romantic content promotes serious, committed love), and no graphic violence, though violence takes place. I really liked it, and would give the author high marks. The only reason I didn't give the full five stars was that, even though Randy was well-drawn, (as were the other, supporting characters) I found him a tad hard to relate to, and I'm not sure why. Maybe differences in social background was part of it, though I'm not usually that much influenced by demographics. (Despite the gender difference, the one character I identified with the most was Alice, the town librarian.)<br /><br />If you're a fan of post-apocalyptic fiction, this should be on your reading list. Ditto if you just like character-driven fiction with a compelling plot and a good set of messages!</span>
  <a data-text-id="2448026441926744267" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_23897005" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23897005" />
    <a title="DeEllen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/931495-deellen"><img alt="DeEllen" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/23897005?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="DeEllen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="DeEllen" href="/user/show/931495-deellen">DeEllen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23897005" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12782392119808961891">This is a letter I wrote to our family after reading this book. This book definitely gives you a lot to think about even if there isn't a nuclear holocaust, we could definitely find ourselves without resources for a number of other reasons. The people in the book had done no planning ahead, but managed to adapt. I think it is important to plan for the worst and hope for the best. Thus came the letter I drafted for our family....<br/><br/>Our Dearest Family,<br/><br/>Current events have given us a good reason to sto</span>
  <span id="freeText12782392119808961891" style="display:none">This is a letter I wrote to our family after reading this book. This book definitely gives you a lot to think about even if there isn't a nuclear holocaust, we could definitely find ourselves without resources for a number of other reasons. The people in the book had done no planning ahead, but managed to adapt. I think it is important to plan for the worst and hope for the best. Thus came the letter I drafted for our family....<br /><br />Our Dearest Family,<br /><br />Current events have given us a good reason to stop and look carefully at our finances and plans for the future. As we have watched the events happening all over the world, we have asked ourselves if we would be prepared for a disaster if one were to occur in our area. FEMA encourages families to get together and come up with a plan for what they would do if a disaster were to occur in their area as well as emergency kits to use in case of a disaster. <br /><br />The emergency plan should include things such as:<br />•	Escape routes<br />•	Evacuation plans<br />•	Family communications<br />•	Utility shut-off and safety<br />•	Insurance and vital records<br />•	Special needs<br />•	Care for pets<br />•	Safety skills<br /><br />Prophets and leaders in our church have counseled families to prepare a year’s worth of food storage to have in case of an emergency. Of course, this food storage is not just for disasters, but also can be used in case of financial difficulties within a family. <br /><br />In 1995, Elder L. Tom Perry of the Quorum of the Twelve Apostles taught:<br />"Acquire and store a reserve of food and supplies that will sustain life. . . . As long as I can remember, we have been taught to prepare for the future and to obtain a year's supply of necessities. I would guess that the years of plenty have almost universally caused us to set aside this counsel. I believe the time to disregard this counsel is over. With events in the world today, it must be considered with all seriousness" ("If Ye Are Prepared Ye Shall Not Fear," Ensign, Nov. 1995, 36).<br /><br />This counsel was given over 10 years ago. As we watch the prices rise continuously at the gas pump and then see the effects of this go to our grocery stores and other items we purchase, we can see that this counsel was not given in vain. Diesel prices are even higher thus posing a problem for truckers who bring in weekly supplies of food, drugs, and other supplies into our valleys. This year, we have felt an urgency to become more self-reliant and prepared in case of a disaster. We know that these preparations will give us a freedom as we will be able to take care of ourselves if we do struggle financially as well as if there came a time when basic utilities (electricity and water) were not available as it is now. We believe that even if circumstances don’t lead us to needing take advantage of these preparations right away, our preparations will lead to a cut in utility bills and healthier living. We would like to extend an invitation to the rest of our family to join us in this quest for self-reliance. <br /><br />We would love to set up a time to have a meeting with each family outside of our own to discuss preparations of 72 hour kits, emergency plans, evacuation plans, gardening, and other skills that lead to self-reliance. We would like to work together as it is always good to have others working with you towards a similar goal.<br /><br />We’d also like to encourage everyone to take some time and do some research on their own. There are some excellent websites to help us begin our quest both from the Church and from our government.<br /><br /> <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.fema.gov/plan/index.shtm">http://www.fema.gov/plan/index.shtm</a><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://lds.org/ldsorg/v/index.jsp?vgnextoid=bbd508f54922d010VgnVCM1000004d82620aRCRD&amp;locale=0&amp;sourceId=25fa2f2324d98010VgnVCM1000004d82620a____">http://lds.org/ldsorg/v/index.jsp?vgn...</a><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.providentliving.org/">http://www.providentliving.org/</a><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://groups.yahoo.com/group/food-storage/">http://groups.yahoo.com/group/food-st...</a><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://providentliving.org/content/display/0,11666,8041-1-4414-1,00.html">http://providentliving.org/content/di...</a><br /><br /><br />We would like to hope that our preparations will not ever be necessary, but we do believe that it is important to plan for the worst and hope for the best. History shows that stable times do not last forever and current events suggest that stable times are soon coming to an end. This can mean a risk to our jobs, limited access to food and electricity, and a lack of ability to live as we are used to living. We have come to a point where we are too reliable on the outside world for our everyday living. Do we want to be prepared if those outside resources suddenly become available, or do we want to find ourselves unable to take care of our most basic needs? We want to be prepared and again, are inviting you, our family, to join us. <br /><br />Lee and DeEllen Stowell<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12782392119808961891" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_112369777" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/112369777" />
    <a title="Zach" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/428808-zach"><img alt="Zach" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536687522p2/428808.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/112369777?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zach" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zach" href="/user/show/428808-zach">Zach</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780553252125" href="/book/show/1037235.Alas_Babylon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/428808-zach?shelf=post-apocalyptic">post-apocalyptic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer112369777" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15827474404181193568">Well first off I have no idea why Day of the Triffids is always held up as the poster child of the <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosy_catastrophe#Cosy_catastrophe' rel='nofollow noopener'> cozy catastrophe</a> phenomenon, because that is kind of this book's whole raison d'etre. What we have here, in fact, is a kind of anti-modern ode to the traditionally-minded small town communities of America, brought about (how else?) by means of the USSR removing the major cities of the nation and replacing them with smoking radioactive craters.<br/><br/>Did I mention this was published in 1959?<br/><br/>As a novel, th</span>
  <span id="freeText15827474404181193568" style="display:none">Well first off I have no idea why Day of the Triffids is always held up as the poster child of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosy_catastrophe#Cosy_catastrophe" rel="nofollow noopener"> cozy catastrophe</a> phenomenon, because that is kind of this book's whole raison d'etre. What we have here, in fact, is a kind of anti-modern ode to the traditionally-minded small town communities of America, brought about (how else?) by means of the USSR removing the major cities of the nation and replacing them with smoking radioactive craters.<br /><br />Did I mention this was published in 1959?<br /><br />As a novel, this is mostly a failure: the coziness of the catastrophe is mostly laughable, the plot kind of plodding, the characters stale and flat, the racism and sexism casual and blatant.<br /><br />As a historical document, though, this is fascinating. For example, Frank clearly thinks of himself (and kind of was, for the time) as a racial progressive: the collapse of civilization quickly renders racism untenable, he tells us: food grown by a black man is just as edible as that provided by a white man, for example. Racism, he understands, is a social construct, and not a function of biology. This doesn't stop him from having his white characters constantly boss his black characters around, though, or writing the dialogue of the latter in broken grammar, and etc. <br /><br />Frank's forward thinking didn't really extend to gender, though. There's a scene toward the end where the protagonist's love interest suggests a plan involving a truck to the men, and then says it would be best if she were the driver. "Like hell you will," says the main character. "It isn't safe for a woman." Now after the past three decades or so, this is a pretty cliche setup that pans out like so:<br /><br />1. woman has idea<br />2. men run with idea but tell woman she has to stay behind for safety<br />3. dumbass men get into trouble<br />4. woman, who has sneakily disobeyed them, saves the day<br />5. men apologize to woman for chauvinist behavior<br />6. characters and audience alike convince themselves that sexism is dead.<br /><br />This is how it plays out in A,B:<br /><br />1. woman has idea<br />2. men run with idea but tell woman she has to stay behind for safety<br />3. woman stays behind like she is told<br /><br /><br />This is to say nothing of the constant griping about bureaucracy and modernism that the characters indulge in ("No tax. No alimony. Let us count our blessings. Never thought I'd see the day.") or the fact that several characters are killed off by radioactive consumer commodities (!) or the continual reminder that what keeps the characters alive after The Day is the knowledge and tools left behind by their pre-modern ancestors.<br /><br />If novel-as-history doesn't appeal to you, I can't really imagine this would be a book worth reading in 2010.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15827474404181193568" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2429915914" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2429915914" />
    <a title="Rob" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57725883-rob"><img alt="Rob" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557030750p2/57725883.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2429915914?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rob" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rob" href="/user/show/57725883-rob">Rob</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/10453231-alas-babylon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57725883-rob?shelf=apocalyptic">apocalyptic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2429915914" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4104551580600774077">The first thing that a reader, new to this book, has to remember is that it was first published in 1959, that’s nearly 60 years ago. This was when the world was in the grip of the, so called, cold war between the USA and USSR. During the Cold War the populous of the world lived in the shadow of a nuclear war. If you are of an age that you can remember the Cuban Missile Crisis of 1962 then you will know what that shadow felt like. At that time, two men, President John F. Kennedy of the USA. and M</span>
  <span id="freeText4104551580600774077" style="display:none">The first thing that a reader, new to this book, has to remember is that it was first published in 1959, that’s nearly 60 years ago. This was when the world was in the grip of the, so called, cold war between the USA and USSR. During the Cold War the populous of the world lived in the shadow of a nuclear war. If you are of an age that you can remember the Cuban Missile Crisis of 1962 then you will know what that shadow felt like. At that time, two men, President John F. Kennedy of the USA. and Mr. Khrushchev of the USSR, held the power to obliterate the entire globe. We of that generation held our collective breath waiting for the bomb to drop and then collectively breathed out when the USSR back down. Crisis averted.<br /><br />Alas Babylon takes a similar scenario but, on this occasion, the crisis is not averted and the bombs do drop.<br /><br />The first thing that occurred to me whilst reading this book was ‘the more things change the more things stay the same’. The holocaust is sparked into being by antagonism between Turkey and Syria. Does this sound familiar to you? It’s now 2018 and they are still at it.<br /><br />The small township of Fort Repose, Florida is somehow spared from total annihilation. Far enough away from major cities and the prevailing wind sending the fallout further north the people of Fort Repose have to come to terms with life in a post nuclear, apocalyptic world. They have to quite literally reinvent the wheel.<br /><br />Living with the populous of Fort Repose for the duration of the book I became totally immersed in their struggle to survive. <br /><br />There is a reason why certain books become classics, it’s because they are bloody good. And this book, believe me, is bloody good. <br /><br />5/5 star recommendation.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4104551580600774077" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_480190842" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/480190842" />
    <a title="Linda Robinson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2613351-linda-robinson"><img alt="Linda Robinson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588091170p2/2613351.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/480190842?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Linda Robinson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Linda Robinson" href="/user/show/2613351-linda-robinson">Linda Robinson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780060812546" href="/book/show/1037236.Alas_Babylon">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer480190842" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3621586916884680890">Frank's book was included in an article on the best post-apocalypse books, and it was the only one I'd not read. The characters are well written, which is what we hope for when the scenario is a given: nuclear holocaust causes chaos. How people react is all there is to write about when the power is out, salt, liquor, batteries, coffee and potable water are history. In this book, ammunition is still around late in the post-game, which seems weird, there's a firefight on a bridge that maybe needed</span>
  <span id="freeText3621586916884680890" style="display:none">Frank's book was included in an article on the best post-apocalypse books, and it was the only one I'd not read. The characters are well written, which is what we hope for when the scenario is a given: nuclear holocaust causes chaos. How people react is all there is to write about when the power is out, salt, liquor, batteries, coffee and potable water are history. In this book, ammunition is still around late in the post-game, which seems weird, there's a firefight on a bridge that maybe needed to be hand to hand. Frank published this in 1959, so there are words I'd prefer never to hear again, and the women are drawn only slightly more powerful than most 50s prototypes, but we're all a product of the time we're set in. The girl Peyton pouts a little about getting female assignments, but she figures out how to overcome. That alone is forward thinking by the author. There are other indications Frank either had hopes for his own offspring, or for humanity in general. I watched The Hunt for Red October this week. Skip Tyler, benched sub driver, remembered helping his father build a bomb shelter in the backyard in the decade set in Alas, Babylon. I remember that time, too. Imagining the book in our neighborhood, I wonder who would have emerged as our clan leader, our inventor; who would have gone rogue, and if I could have taken a role as a young girl I could be proud of today. And I wonder if the bomb shelter in the LaLondes' backyard is still operational, or if the new home owners even know it's there.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3621586916884680890" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1560940297" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1560940297" />
    <a title="WarpDrive" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16160897-warpdrive"><img alt="WarpDrive" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605312701p2/16160897.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1560940297?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="WarpDrive" class="user" itemprop="url" name="WarpDrive" href="/user/show/16160897-warpdrive">WarpDrive</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16160897-warpdrive?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1560940297" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer222977658978713432">This is a compelling, atmospheric book about the physical, social and emotional consequences of nuclear war in a small Florida community. <br/>This book was written in 1959, when the dangers of a nuclear conflagration were not negligible, but it feels surprisingly modern and relevant to modern sensibilities. <br/>The crescendo of tension bringing to the actual trigger of the nuclear catastrophe, as well as the description of the event itself, are quite memorable and feel very realistic – unsettlingly so. </span>
  <span id="freeText222977658978713432" style="display:none">This is a compelling, atmospheric book about the physical, social and emotional consequences of nuclear war in a small Florida community. <br />This book was written in 1959, when the dangers of a nuclear conflagration were not negligible, but it feels surprisingly modern and relevant to modern sensibilities. <br />The crescendo of tension bringing to the actual trigger of the nuclear catastrophe, as well as the description of the event itself, are quite memorable and feel very realistic – unsettlingly so. The description of the immediate after-effects of a global nuclear war, although a tad optimistic in my opinion, is also quite realistic and unsettling in its ordinariness. <br />On the not-so-positive side, the initial build-up is at times a bit slow, the ending is a bit underwhelming and too optimistic for my taste, and there are a couple of moments where the 50's culture comes to the surface, in the form of open sexism that, sadly, was still quite present in society at the time: this statement by the main character: "The more he learned about women the more there was to learn except that he had learned this: they needed a man around" caused literally to boil my blood. <br />Overall, however, it is a well written, compelling, recommended SF classic that is still now one of the best examples of this particular post-apocalypse survival story genre. 3.5 stars, rounded up to 4. </span>
  <a data-text-id="222977658978713432" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1560940297">

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/22477591" />
    <a title="Sandi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/811687-sandi"><img alt="Sandi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404709147p2/811687.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14695022919380432932">I read "Alas, Babylon" during a vacation to Mammoth at the end of August, 2005. We had no TV, no newspapers and no radio for 4 days. When we got home, we learned about the destruction caused by Hurricane Katrina. As I watched news reports about the aftermath, I kept thinking back to the incidents in this book. It was just a bit spooky and it made me realize how unprepared I am for a major disaster. It also made me wonder if it's even possible to be truly prepared for a disaster. Maybe it's your </span>
  <span id="freeText14695022919380432932" style="display:none">I read "Alas, Babylon" during a vacation to Mammoth at the end of August, 2005. We had no TV, no newspapers and no radio for 4 days. When we got home, we learned about the destruction caused by Hurricane Katrina. As I watched news reports about the aftermath, I kept thinking back to the incidents in this book. It was just a bit spooky and it made me realize how unprepared I am for a major disaster. It also made me wonder if it's even possible to be truly prepared for a disaster. Maybe it's your character and intelligence that determines if you survive and how you live afterwards.</span>

<div id="review_3083649598" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3083649598" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/695116-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1340192527p2/695116.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3083649598?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/695116-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781455883400" href="/book/show/15022646-alas-babylon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=2fiction">2fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=apocalyptic">apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=1audio">1audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=historical">historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3083649598" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18015158591051120650">This book is 60 years old, but well worth reading & not just for its influence on the genre. It's one of the first to deal with a post apocalyptic world after a nuclear war, so it's not pretty, but it is instructive on many levels & an entertaining read. It should be a Thanksgiving Day read in the US because it shows how much we have to be grateful for: electricity, clean water delivered to the tap, good food, medicines, medical care, & relative order.<br/><br/>Frank really sets the scene well. Fort Repos</span>
  <span id="freeText18015158591051120650" style="display:none">This book is 60 years old, but well worth reading &amp; not just for its influence on the genre. It's one of the first to deal with a post apocalyptic world after a nuclear war, so it's not pretty, but it is instructive on many levels &amp; an entertaining read. It should be a Thanksgiving Day read in the US because it shows how much we have to be grateful for: electricity, clean water delivered to the tap, good food, medicines, medical care, &amp; relative order.<br /><br />Frank really sets the scene well. Fort Repose reminds me a lot of the small town where I lived on the Eastern Shore of Maryland. It's very southern &amp; I lived there in the mid 60s, so not long after this book was published. The racial issues were about the same, almost a complete separation between races, although the public school was desegregated when I went there. Not everyone was happy about that on either side. There's a telling sentence when Randy, one of the least prejudiced characters, realizes the new school will be desegregated.<br /><br />I really liked the way Mark explains the situation to Randy. Not only does it fill me in on the state of the world, but it does so in a logical way that eases the data dump. The seeds of MAD are clearly shown as are the reasons behind JFK's space race. Later, the retired admiral expands a bit on this pointing out how all the upper level thinking was tactical, none strategic. They're important points to remember for the last bit of the novel. Wow! Frank really knocks the point home.<br /><br />As I read through this, I had a feeling Frank could have gone into a lot more detail, but I'm glad he didn't. He addressed the main points &amp; let characterization fill in much of the rest. Some people rose to the occasion, some couldn't live with it, others just slowly decayed. Florence was a hoot &amp; a great hook to explain so much of the everyday life. While there was greed &amp; some violence, the book never resorted to gore or outrageous action. It was always real people dealing with a very scary &amp; realistic situation. It highlights how unprepared &amp; useless the 'best' of society could be &amp; how well prepared some of the 'least' were, especially the so-called second class citizens of the day. The need for some specialists, such as a doctor were also well displayed.<br /><br />I've read quite a few post apocalyptic novels over the years. Many are nothing more than action-porn, entertaining, but not not particularly filling. If you like this, I highly recommend <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4922079.One_Second_After__After___1_" title="One Second After (After, #1) by William R. Forstchen" rel="nofollow noopener">One Second After</a> an update of this book &amp; <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1689657.World_Made_by_Hand__World_Made_by_Hand__1_" title="World Made by Hand (World Made by Hand #1) by James Howard Kunstler" rel="nofollow noopener">World Made by Hand</a> which shows it doesn't take a cataclysmic event to bring down civilization. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38180.On_the_Beach" title="On the Beach by Nevil Shute" rel="nofollow noopener">On the Beach</a> was written a few year later than <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38169.Alas__Babylon" title="Alas, Babylon by Pat Frank" rel="nofollow noopener">Alas, Babylon</a> about a much worse nuclear war. About a decade before, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/93269.Earth_Abides" title="Earth Abides by George R. Stewart" rel="nofollow noopener">Earth Abides</a> shows a world where almost all the humans die due to a disease, an update of Shelley's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/966835.The_Last_Man" title="The Last Man by Mary Wollstonecraft Shelley" rel="nofollow noopener">The Last Man</a> &amp; far more readable.<br /><br />This was my first time reading it as an audiobook &amp; it was wonderful. I read this this time with the Evolution of SF group here:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/20881124">https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/...</a><br />I last read this in 2013 as a PB. My 5 star review is here:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/51392664">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18015158591051120650" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1329616255" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1329616255" />
    <a title="Ioana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1721356-ioana"><img alt="Ioana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505655996p2/1721356.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1329616255?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ioana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ioana" href="/user/show/1721356-ioana">Ioana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1721356-ioana?shelf=dystopic-fiction">dystopic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1721356-ioana?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1721356-ioana?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1721356-ioana?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1329616255" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16984376941465978840"><b>2.5 stars, rounded up </b><br/>As a historical relic, <i>Alas, Babylon</i> is fascinating. Written just as things were heating up during the Cold War, it displays a level of paranoia that seems outrageous/laughable today, depending on your disposition. Still, I have no doubt it's accurate as a gauge for the mood of swaths of the public at the time (I'm old enough to remember the 80s from the "other side" of the world - in Bucharest, we lived in fear of nuclear fallout from any bombings unleashed on the USSR, an</span>
  <span id="freeText16984376941465978840" style="display:none"><b>2.5 stars, rounded up </b><br />As a historical relic, <i>Alas, Babylon</i> is fascinating. Written just as things were heating up during the Cold War, it displays a level of paranoia that seems outrageous/laughable today, depending on your disposition. Still, I have no doubt it's accurate as a gauge for the mood of swaths of the public at the time (I'm old enough to remember the 80s from the "other side" of the world - in Bucharest, we lived in fear of nuclear fallout from any bombings unleashed on the USSR, and that was when the Cold War was winding down).<br /><br />The quick synopsis: the USSR bombs the US to pieces, and there are enclaves of people trying to survive in these "contaminated areas", without electricity, trash collection, trade, communications, etc. The writing is good, the pace is excellent, the story is spell-binding, the characterizations are realistic.<br /><br />I actually enjoyed <i>Alas, Babylon</i> quite a bit (I might even say a 5/5), but I have other important criteria too, like novelty, creativity, lyricism, profundity, etc. <br /><br />This book is incredible as a historical window into the past, but Frank adheres to all known stereotypes as he presents a United States imagined by the most shallow, inane, unthinking, sheep-like indicators available. It's like if a person in the year 3000 found a copy of the <i>Life of the Kardashians</i>; it'd probably be a fascinating period study into our time, but not the most illuminating of our potential for human thought and possibility.<br /><br />Some examples: as a book written in 1959 about the deep south (Florida), <i>Alas, Babylon</i> is pretty blatantly racist. Though, at least, not militantly so, at a time when violence against non-whites was common; Frank does present his white characters as tolerant, and writes about their solidarity with the black inhabitants of Fort-Repose. Still, this "tolerance" is quite paternalistic and thus, condescending; he's like those people today who "have black friends" so they don't consider themselves racist... Second, ditto on women. Frank goes to some lengths to portray women as independent, yet in the next breaths pats himself on the head for being so forward-looking while restating how women still "need" men. <br /><br />In both dimensions of race and gender, Frank probably was indeed "ahead" or at least in line with the times - he recognizes non-whites and women are people, have (or should have) equal rights, and should be treated with dignity, yet his very tone undermines his points. Like the white guy who says, almost surprised, "she's a woman, and she's so independent and strong" or "he's black, and he's so responsible and intelligent"... <br /><br />Then, of course, there's the paranoia, as mentioned previously, the absolutely blinding patriotism and misapprehension of "The Other". And, throughout it all, we are bombarded with typically heart-warming, cliched, interpersonal moments of connection and fierce moments of American independence. <i>We can do it, together! We can rebuild! We will work as the independent idealists that we are to make America great again!</i> And so on, and the like. This is like, PERFECT Cold War propaganda.<br /><br />Also, unrelatedly to any critical analysis, the book also seemed quite superficial because it skimmed over so much time. <i>Alas, Babylon</i> paints a vague portrait of a post-apocalyptic world, but there's not quite enough there to get a solid sense of the entire picture, and the reader is left craving so much more in the end.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16984376941465978840" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_609572409" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/609572409" />
    <a title="Kelly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/773729-kelly"><img alt="Kelly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1541106076p2/773729.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/609572409?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kelly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kelly" href="/user/show/773729-kelly">Kelly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/773729-kelly?shelf=sf">sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/773729-kelly?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/773729-kelly?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer609572409" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15781577451007374719">"The classic apocalyptic novel that stunned the world" is across the front cover of this book. It was written in 1959 when fear of the atomic bomb was all-encompassing so I can only imagine how stunned people were to read this book when it came out. <br/><br/>The last apocalyptic novel I read was <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6288.The_Road' title='The Road by Cormac McCarthy' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Road</a> and it was incredibly depressing. This one wasn't...while at times this book was chilling, it was filled with goodness and hope. In fact, it was a mirror image of the way I've always pictured things woul</span>
  <span id="freeText15781577451007374719" style="display:none">"The classic apocalyptic novel that stunned the world" is across the front cover of this book. It was written in 1959 when fear of the atomic bomb was all-encompassing so I can only imagine how stunned people were to read this book when it came out. <br /><br />The last apocalyptic novel I read was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6288.The_Road" title="The Road by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">The Road</a> and it was incredibly depressing. This one wasn't...while at times this book was chilling, it was filled with goodness and hope. In fact, it was a mirror image of the way I've always pictured things would go down in case of a nuclear war. Well, maybe not exactly a mirror image; the author included many things I hadn't really thought about and, believe me, I did a lot of thinking while reading this book. I don't really think it will ever come down to this kind of global catastrophe, but I've definitely done a lot of thinking while reading this book...and I'm still thinking. Due to this fact I gave it 5 stars instead of 4.<br /><br />**POTENTIAL SPOILERS BELOW**<br /><br />The lawlessness scared me the most. That being said, I truly believe the majority of people would be like those in this book...neighbors/family/friends/communities helping one another. There were some who were too weak (physically/mentally/emotionally) to survive the shock long enough to stabilize into a "new normal". I truly think my neighborhood would pull together like the main characters in this book...into a little tribe, everyone finding their niche and providing for some need of the community. They had to protect each other from those who would steal from or hurt them. I thought it was interesting that after a manner of months they settled into a routine and everything went back to how it used to be without goods being shipped via plane/train/truck...they ate things in season, whatever was growing/living where they lived at the immediate time. They had to figure out where to get salt, how to use things they had to create other things they needed. It was very interesting to read.<br /><br />I think this quote from the commentary at the end of the book by Hal Hager sums this book up very well:<br /><br />"...Alas, Babylon was to be among the first to give a public voice to the fears and anxieties associated with a nuclear attack and with threats of atomic radiation. And unlike others in the genre, he offered unmitigated hope. Randy Bragg and company not only survive the devastation that leaves vast Contaminated Zones throughout the United States, but they also apply the best in themselves to begin the re-establishment of life within a civil society. All of the atomic fears of the 1950s (and later) are there, but Frank's 'message' seems to be that we can survive even the worst catastrophe with everyday, secularized applications of faith, hope, and charity."</span>
  <a data-text-id="15781577451007374719" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_809569462" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/809569462" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3335664-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1551667298p2/3335664.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/809569462?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/3335664-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/10453231-alas-babylon">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Small-town Floridians, jezebels with radioactive jewelry</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=1950s">1950s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=post-apocalyptic">post-apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=modern-classic">modern-classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=american-literature">american-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=survival">survival</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=florida">florida</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer809569462" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13272287398830380376">Pat Frank's <i>Alas, Babylon</i> is a classic novel of post-nuclear war survival. Set in Fort Repose, Florida, a tiny town that is missed by the nuclear missiles that level all major cities in the U.S., it is less Cold War science fiction than a survivalist epic.<br/><br/>The author of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/776112726' rel='nofollow noopener'>One Second After</a> acknowledged this book as one of his inspirations, and the two books are very similar in many ways. Both feature the residents of a small Southern town forming a survivalist community in the wake of the collapse o</span>
  <span id="freeText13272287398830380376" style="display:none">Pat Frank's <i>Alas, Babylon</i> is a classic novel of post-nuclear war survival. Set in Fort Repose, Florida, a tiny town that is missed by the nuclear missiles that level all major cities in the U.S., it is less Cold War science fiction than a survivalist epic.<br /><br />The author of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/776112726" rel="nofollow noopener">One Second After</a> acknowledged this book as one of his inspirations, and the two books are very similar in many ways. Both feature the residents of a small Southern town forming a survivalist community in the wake of the collapse of the U.S. government and technological civilization. In <i>Alas, Babylon</i>, it is a nuclear war between the US and the USSR, the ominous and inevitable build-up to war taking up the first half of the book, as only a few people realize just what is unfolding before them on the news.<br /><br />As in <i>One Second After</i>, <i>Alas, Babylon</i> features an All-American protagonist stepping up to take charge because no one else will, while he tries to manage his small family (in this case, the family of his brother, an Air Force officer who knew what was going down and sent them to relative safety ahead of time). There are food shortages, the necessity of modern people figuring out how to survive without modern technology, the return of the barter economy, as well as bandits and highwaymen. As a survivalist epic, it's not as grim as it could have been, but it's another one of those books that might make you think about stocking up on bullets and beans, just in case.<br /><br />For a book written in 1959, <i>Alas, Babylon</i> holds up surprisingly well, largely because as with all stories about a total collapse of civilization, once the grid goes down and there is no more government, it doesn't matter whether it was 1959 or 1980 or 2014, everything is going to look like the 19th century pretty quickly. The USSR is no longer, but Russia still has missiles pointed at us; nuclear war may no longer be as likely as it once seemed, but it's hardly a threat that's vanished. The black characters, despite living in Florida in 1959, are treated better by the author than in some more recent post-apocalyptic novels I could name.<br /><br />This was a good read for anyone who's a fan of survivalist novels and stories about what a community would do after the end of the world. Very slightly dated, but the writing style and the challenges facing the characters will mostly keep you from noticing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13272287398830380376" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2645671906" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2645671906" />
    <a title="Dawn C" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14948447-dawn-c"><img alt="Dawn C" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535435406p2/14948447.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2645671906?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dawn C" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dawn C" href="/user/show/14948447-dawn-c">Dawn C</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/11994977-alas-babylon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14948447-dawn-c?shelf=media-audible">media-audible</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2645671906" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1276819178724339013">Following a small Florida community’s survival after an atomic bomb ravages their Florida state homes, this is a surprisingly uplifting piece of post apocalyptic fiction. Don’t expect to read a real life account of living with the horrific effects of the bomb, as their area is pretty much on the outskirts and sheltered from radiation and the severe consequences thereof, even if it’s briefly mentioned. Horrible things do happen, but they’re off-screen or on-sceeen brought on by the people who liv</span>
  <span id="freeText1276819178724339013" style="display:none">Following a small Florida community’s survival after an atomic bomb ravages their Florida state homes, this is a surprisingly uplifting piece of post apocalyptic fiction. Don’t expect to read a real life account of living with the horrific effects of the bomb, as their area is pretty much on the outskirts and sheltered from radiation and the severe consequences thereof, even if it’s briefly mentioned. Horrible things do happen, but they’re off-screen or on-sceeen brought on by the people who live around our MCs. They don’t really have any political view on who did this to them or why, nor is the focus of the story. However, it’s written in 1959, and while it shows, it’s definitely also a piece of speculative fiction trying to burst out of of the limitations of its time, and I admire that a lot. The novel is a great, unsentimental testiment of community among human beings brought together by circumstance, how they are willing to help and provide for each other. I found it very engaging and matter-of-factly written which is how I seemingly prefer it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1276819178724339013" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Tyo8FUZUTb5MdUhJlynPx1Lp28GVd8h3Il25jdywiSd1DFXH/QqE1ute/Chg3MUYhLY8CVv6RhB5I/88EfN7vQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('L7j/7uux5Fdg1njCmTqPOdF6NsDp4ssqeYUmgfCceZEVnpY8UO8tP8f9zKNuz4XmByXRCCdvRU0i+2AwPd+LCw==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Brian and Louise meet during their college days at Cal Poly State University in San Luis Obispo and embark on a pursuit of a calling to the nations that propels them from the Navajo Nation\'s painted desert in Arizona to the wild steppes of Central Asia. Along their way five children join the cross-cultural roller coaster. Disarmingly honest and charmingly humorous, their tale will thrill you and bring tears to your eyes. An intensely personal memoir, this book still manages to pack a powerful dose of missionary insight and Biblical principles for seeing the Church explode into life among peoples that have never even heard of Jesus. Winter, Founder, U.S. Center for World Mission\n\nI wept, laughed and was stirred by this book. I love a good story, and this is a really good one! I wholeheartedly recommend this book as it not only tells an amazing story, but also becomes a discipleship tool that reveals to us a whole new paradigm of church and missions. --- David Broodryk, Kingdom People Network, South Africa\n\nIf you want a radically cross-cultural journey without leaving your favorite easy chair - this book is your ticket.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/21475.Pat_Frank">About Pat Frank</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12543270038787442242">"Pat Frank" was the lifelong nickname adopted by the American writer, newspaperman, and government consultant, who was born Harry Hart Frank and who is remembered today almost exclusively for his post-apocalyptic novel <i>Alas, Babylon</i>. Before the publication of his first novel <i>Mr. Adam</i> launched his second career as novelist and independent writer, Frank spent many years as a journalist and informati</span>
  <span id="freeText12543270038787442242" style="display:none">"Pat Frank" was the lifelong nickname adopted by the American writer, newspaperman, and government consultant, who was born Harry Hart Frank and who is remembered today almost exclusively for his post-apocalyptic novel <i>Alas, Babylon</i>. Before the publication of his first novel <i>Mr. Adam</i> launched his second career as novelist and independent writer, Frank spent many years as a journalist and information handler for several newspapers, agencies, and government bureaus. His fiction and nonfiction books, stories, and articles made good use of his years of experience observing government and military bureaucracy and its malfunctions, and the threat of nuclear proliferation and annihilation. After the success of <i>Alas, Babylon</i>, Frank concentrated on writing for magazines and journals, putting his beliefs and concerns to political use, and advising various government bodies. In 1960 he served as a member of the Democratic National Committee. In 1961, the year in which he received an American Heritage Foundation Award, he was consultant to the National Aeronautics and Space Council. From 1963 through 1964 the Department of Defense made use of Frank's expertise and advice, and this consultancy turned out to be his last response to his country's call. His other books include <i>Mr. Adam</i> and <i>Forbidden Area</i>. <br /><br />Biography courtesy of HarperCollins.com</span>
  <a data-text-id="12543270038787442242" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
